These results demonstrate the benefit of our novel approach, combining comparative cytogenomics and hybrid sequencing technologies, especially for species for which genetic maps are not available.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly for both S. intermedia clones</h3><p>The assembly of PacBio reads for clone 7747 is more fragmented than the assembly of ON/Illumina reads for clone 8410. It displays a higher percentage of ambiguous bases (0.003%) and a lower coverage (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#Tab1">1</a>). Several draft contigs representing contamination from plastids, mitochondria, and a virus (Human alpha herpesvirus 1 strain) were detected which show largely increased coverages of 2925-, 17,126- and 73-fold respectively. These overrepresented sequences caused a diminished overall coverage in both assemblies. Canu performs best with more than 50X coverage, while lower values might decrease the contiguity of an assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.215087.116&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2465">32</a></sup>.</p><p>Comparing read length N50 of the two S. intermedia clones, the ON library of clone 8410 (16,322 kb) yielded a larger amount of long reads than clone 7747 with an N50 of 9879 kb. As a consequence, longer repeats may not be sequenced completely, leading to a more fragmented assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Chakraborty, M., Baldwin-Brown, J. G., Long, A. D. &amp; Emerson, J. J. Contiguous and accurate de novo assembly of metazoan genomes with modest long read coverage. Nucl. Acids Res. 44, e147. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkw654&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2472">44</a></sup>, as in case of clone 7747.</p><p>In summary, the PacBio read assembly of <i>S.intermedia</i> 7747 provides continuous pseudomolecules but these might comprise ambiguous and low coverage nucleotide stretches, and possibly missing sequences, as described for other plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Jiao, W. B. et al. Improving and correcting the contiguity of long-read genome assemblies of three plant species using optical mapping and chromosome conformation capture data. Genome Res. 27, 778–786. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.213652.116&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2482">45</a></sup>. Despite the lower library quality for clone 7747, the non-hybrid PacBio assembly shows (as expected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Jayakumar, V. &amp; Sakakibara, Y. Comprehensive evaluation of non-hybrid genome assembly tools for third-generation PacBio long-read sequence data. Br. Bioinform. 20, 866–876. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bib/bbx147&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2486">46</a></sup>) good results for the scaffold N50 (8.3 Mbp similar to 9.25 of the ON/Illumina assembly, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#Tab1">1</a>), depicting the synergy of cytogenetics and long read sequencing technologies.</p><p>The distribution of functional eggNOG groups within the <i>S. intermedia</i> clones and <i>S. polyrhiza</i> is largely balanced, indicating a high similarity in overall functional gene content between both species which amounts with ~ 20,000 genes, a rather low number compared to most land plants. The BUSCO results reflect the increased fragmentation of the 7747 assembly by an increased number of fragmented and missing genes (~ 20% in total). With 3.0% of fragmented and 3.9% of missing candidate genes, the ON based assembly of clone 8410 exhibits a good representation of the expected gene content. It has to be noted, that the embryophyta set of orthologue sequences (mostly from land plants) might not be optimal for aquatic plants, because unnecessary genes might have been lost during evolutionary organ reduction and aquatic life style of duckweeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Commun. 5, 3311. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms4311&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2502">21</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Repetitive elements</h3><p><i>S. intermedia</i> revealed a rather low proportion of detected repeats (~ 25%), in comparison with other genomes of similar size, such as that of <i>A. thaliana</i> (157 Mbp/1C; 32%<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Maumus, F. &amp; Quesneville, H. Deep investigation of Arabidopsis thaliana junk DNA reveals a continuum between repetitive elements and genomic dark matter. PLoS ONE 9, e94101. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0094101&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2519">47</a></sup> ), or the even smaller genome of <i>Genlisea nigrocaulis</i> (86 Mbp/1C) with 15.9% of total repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Vu, G. T. H. et al. Comparative genome analysis reveals divergent genome size evolution in a carnivorous plant genus. Plant Genome 8, 3. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3835/plantgenome2015.04.0021&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2526">48</a></sup>. Similar repeat proportions were reported by Wang et al. (2014)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Commun. 5, 3311. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms4311&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2530">21</a></sup> for <i>S. polyrhiza</i>, however, later Michael et al. (2017)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Michael, T. P. et al. Comprehensive definition of genome features in Spirodela polyrhiza by high-depth physical mapping and short-read DNA sequencing strategies. Plant J. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13400&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2538">25</a></sup> reported a higher proportion of mobile elements (25%) for that species. Possible explanations might be: (1) There is a difference in repeat content between <i>S. polyrhiza</i> and <i>S. intermedia;</i> this seems less likely, because of the close relationship and same genome size of both species. (2) The assembly by Michael et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Michael, T. P. et al. Comprehensive definition of genome features in Spirodela polyrhiza by high-depth physical mapping and short-read DNA sequencing strategies. Plant J. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13400&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2548">25</a></sup> is more complete; this is probably not the case, because we found a lower content of mobile elements also in unassembled reads. (3) Their methods including structure-based detection revealed low copy elements (one or a few copies/C) which are not captured by our clustering and similarity-based searches. (4) Michael et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Michael, T. P. et al. Comprehensive definition of genome features in Spirodela polyrhiza by high-depth physical mapping and short-read DNA sequencing strategies. Plant J. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13400&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2552">25</a></sup> described a ratio of soloLTRs to complete elements of 8 to 1. Because our tools did not determine soloLTRs, the repeat content for <i>S. intermedia</i> might be underestimated. A high proportion of soloLTRs suggests that the genome size of Spirodela decreased by deletion-biased DNA double-strand repair<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Schubert, I. &amp; Vu, G. T. H. Genome stability and evolution: attempting a holistic view. Trends Plant Sci. 21, 749–757. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tplants.2016.06.003&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2560">49</a></sup> via the single-strand annealing pathway since its separation from the other duckweed lineages. Remarkable is the high abundance of SSR repeats, especially that of dispersed (AG)<sub>n</sub> microsatellite arrays, which in rice contribute to regulate gene expression by binding transcription factors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Gong, R. et al. Divergent functions of the GAGA-binding transcription factor family in rice. Plant J. 94, 32–47. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13837&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2566">50</a></sup>. Such arrays were reported also for <i>S. polyrhiza</i>, where they “severely impeded elongation during sequence assembly”<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Commun. 5, 3311. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms4311&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2573">21</a></sup>.</p><p>The unusually low copy number of both 45S and 5S rDNA repeats revealed in the genome of <i>S. polyrhiza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hoang, P. N. T. et al. Generating a high-confidence reference genome map of the Greater Duckweed by integration of cytogenomic, optical mapping and Oxford Nanopore technologies. Plant J. 96, 670–684. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.14049&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2582">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Michael, T. P. et al. Comprehensive definition of genome features in Spirodela polyrhiza by high-depth physical mapping and short-read DNA sequencing strategies. Plant J. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13400&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2585">25</a></sup>, inspired curiosity about the number and arrangement of those genes in related <i>S. intermedia</i>. The qPCR-based estimation showed ~ 93 copies of the 25S rRNA genes for both Si8410 and Si7747 genomes, a number very close to the estimate for <i>S. polyrhiza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Michael, T. P. et al. Comprehensive definition of genome features in Spirodela polyrhiza by high-depth physical mapping and short-read DNA sequencing strategies. Plant J. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13400&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2594">25</a></sup><i>.</i> Unlike <i>S. polyrhiza</i>, where the 45S rDNA was shown to locate in a single chromosome locus, our deep coverage ON sequencing of Si8410 revealed at least two 45S rDNA loci, located at ~ 83 kb upstream of the telomere at SiChr01, and at ~ 8 kb upstream of the telomere at SiChr06. The fact that FISH revealed only one signal on SiChr01, suggests that this location is the major 45S rDNA locus with the majority of the ~ 93 gene repeats. However, the exact 45S rDNA copy number distribution between the two loci remains unclear, because no read-through sequences are available. For the 5S rDNA, a range of generated ON read-through sequences, revealed two distinct loci, one containing a cluster of 31 repeats on SiChr15 which has been also visualized by FISH, and a second split locus on SiChr14 containing two clusters with 7 and 13 repeats, separated by ~ 13 kb long region of non-rDNA sequence (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#Fig6">6</a>b). While the general arrangement of the 5S rRNA genes in <i>S. intermedia</i> resembles that of the <i>S. polyrhiza</i>, the total 5S rDNA copy number in <i>S. intermedia</i> is even lower, 51 vs 73 in <i>S. polyrhiza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hoang, P. N. T. et al. Generating a high-confidence reference genome map of the Greater Duckweed by integration of cytogenomic, optical mapping and Oxford Nanopore technologies. Plant J. 96, 670–684. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.14049&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2618">24</a></sup>. This is the smallest number reported so far for any plant species. Usually, the copy number of 5S RNA genes in land plants varies from 2000 to 75,000<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wicke, S., Costa, A., Munoz, J. &amp; Quandt, D. Restless 5S: the re-arrangement(s) and evolution of the nuclear ribosomal DNA in land plants. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 61, 321–332. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ympev.2011.06.023&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2623">51</a></sup>. Therefore, our findings for <i>S. polyrhiza</i> and <i>S. intermedia</i> proclaim that the small copy number of rDNA is a unique phenomenon of the genus Spirodela.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Material and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Material and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Plant material</h3><p><i>Spirodela intermedia</i> W. Koch (accessions 8410 from Panama City and 7747 from Lima, Peru) were obtained from Elias Landolt’s collection via Klaus Appenroth, University of Jena and Rutgers Duckweed Stock Cooperative (New Jersey, USA). The fronds were grown in liquid nutrient medium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Appenroth, K.-J., Teller, S. &amp; Horn, M. Photophysiology of turion formation and germination in Spirodela polyrhiza. Biol. Plantarum 38, 95–106. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/bf02879642&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (1996)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2648">52</a></sup> under 16 h white light of 100 µmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at 24 °C.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genomic DNA isolation</h3><p>Genomic DNA of <i>S. intermedia</i> (clone 7747) was extracted from fresh fronds using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen) for PacBio sequencing. High molecular weight DNA of <i>S. intermedia</i> (clone 8410) was isolated for Oxford Nanopore and Illumina sequencing as follows: the plants were kept three days in the darkness. After harvesting, 10 g of the fronds were used for DNA isolation from purified nuclei according to the protocol of Vondrak et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Vondrak, T. et al. Characterization of repeat arrays in ultra-long nanopore reads reveals frequent origin of satellite DNA from retrotransposon-derived tandem repeats. Plant J. 101, 484–500. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.14546&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2670">53</a></sup>, with two minor modifications: the centrifugation of nuclei was performed at 650 × g instead of 200 × g due to their small size, and 2 × CTAB isolation buffer was supplemented with 2% PVP-360 (polyvinylpyrrolidone, avg. molecular weight 360,000).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome sequencing</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">PacBio</h4><p>After shearing of genomic DNA (<i>S. intermedia</i> clone 7747), a size-selected 20 kb library was sequenced on the Pacific Biosciences RS II platform (GATC Biotech, Konstanz, Germany) combining the P6-C4 polymerase-chemistry and 240 min of movie duration. Two rounds of sequencing resulted in 149 Gb of raw read data.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec19">Oxford nanopore</h4><p>The sequencing libraries were prepared from 3 μg of purified HMW DNA using a Ligation Sequencing Kit SQK-LSK109 (Oxford Nanopore Technologies) as described by Vondrak et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Vondrak, T. et al. Characterization of repeat arrays in ultra-long nanopore reads reveals frequent origin of satellite DNA from retrotransposon-derived tandem repeats. Plant J. 101, 484–500. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.14546&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2697">53</a></sup>. Briefly, the DNA was treated with 2 μl of NEBNext FFPE DNA Repair Mix and 3 μl of NEBNext Ultra II End-prep enzyme mix in a 60 μl volume that also included 3.5 μl of FFPE and 3.5 μl of End-prep reaction buffers (New England Biolabs). The reaction was performed at 20 °C for 5 min and 65 °C for 5 min. Then, the DNA was purified using a 0.4 × volume of AMPure XP beads (Beckman Coulter). Because long DNA fragments caused clumping of the beads and were difficult to detach, the elution was performed with 3 mM TRIS–HCl (pH 8.5) and was extended up to 40 min. Subsequent steps including adapter ligation using NEBNext Quick T4 DNA Ligase and library preparation for sequencing were performed as recommended. The whole library was loaded onto FLO-MIN106 R9.4 flow cell and sequenced on MinION instrument until the number of active pores dropped below 40 (21–24 h). Basecalling of the raw reads was done using Albacore 2.3.3 (Oxford Nanopore Technologies).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec20">Illumina</h4><p>Illumina sequencing was performed by Admera Health, LLC (South Plainfield, NJ, USA) using a KAPA DNA Library kit (Roche) and resulted in 130 million paired-end reads (2 × 150 nt).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Sequence assembly and pseudomolecule construction</h3><p>After an initial filtering for potential bacterial contamination (blastn against microbial NCBI refseq database from Aug 2017) and minimum read length (500 nucleotides), PacBio reads were assembled using the Canu pipeline v. 1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.215087.116&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2718">32</a></sup> (options: ‘genomeSize = 160 m, correctedErrorRate = 0.105′) consisting of the following steps:</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">(1)</span>
                    
                      <p>Trimming, error correction and contig construction: Reads were corrected and trimmed by comparing overlaps. A minimum length of 500 nucleotides and a maximum error rate of 10.5% was chosen for extending a contig. Only reads consisting of more than 1000 nucleotides in length were considered in this step. Afterwards, the corrected reads were trimmed to improve overall read quality by using overlap information to detect high confidence regions. Contigs of insufficient read coverage and/or containing ‘noisy’ sequence were categorized as ‘unsupported regions’ and divided at weak sequence positions into subcontigs with higher support.</p>
                      <p>After further contig construction on the basis of overlaps, a consensus sequence was built by removing the remaining sequencing errors to raise the overall assembly quality.</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">(2)</span>
                    
                      <p><i>Scaffolding and gap filling</i></p>
                      <p>In a first round of scaffolding, the two genomes of the sister species <i>S. polyrhiza</i> (from clones 9505 and 7498)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Cao, H. X. et al. The map-based genome sequence of Spirodela polyrhiza aligned with its chromosomes, a reference for karyotype evolution. New Phytol. 209, 354–363. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/nph.13592&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2753">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Michael, T. P. et al. Comprehensive definition of genome features in Spirodela polyrhiza by high-depth physical mapping and short-read DNA sequencing strategies. Plant J. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13400&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2756">25</a></sup> were used as references for Mauve Genome Aligner v20150522<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Darling, A. C., Mau, B., Blattner, F. R. &amp; Perna, N. T. Mauve: multiple alignment of conserved genomic sequence with rearrangements. Genome Res. 14, 1394–1403. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.2289704&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2760">33</a></sup> to order contigs. Scaffolding was performed by SSPACE-Longread v.1-1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinform. 15, 211. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-15-211&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2764">34</a></sup> (default options). The resulting scaffold assembly was used for the super-scaffolding approach. For this aim, contigs were assigned to 18 putative pseudomolecules (corresponding to the 18 <i>S. intermedia</i> chromosomes) using the information of cross-FISH of 93 <i>S. polyrhiza</i> BACs on the chromosomes of <i>S. intermedia</i> clone 8410<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hoang, P. T. N. &amp; Schubert, I. Reconstruction of chromosome rearrangements between the two most ancestral duckweed species Spirodela polyrhiza and S. intermedia. Chromosoma 126, 729–739. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00412-017-0636-7&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2778">29</a></sup>. New cytogenetic probes using BACs from the genomic regions of interest were designed for FISH experiments to approve localization of the contigs within the pseudomolecules and to resolve mis-assemblies. Additionally, bacterial contamination was filtered as described previously.</p>
                    
                  </li>
                </ol><p>The quality of both <i>S. intermedia</i> genome assemblies was assessed by the BUSCO program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btv351&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2791">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 543–548. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx319&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2794">37</a></sup> with the Embryophyta odb10 dataset comprising 1375 conserved genes.</p><p>The ONT/Illumina assembly for clone 8410 was performed using MaSuRCA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the MaSuRCA mega-reads algorithm. Genome Res. 27, 787–792. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.213405.116&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2801">54</a></sup>. In the first step, super-reads were assembled from 68 million Illumina 2 × 151 nt paired-end reads (128-fold coverage). Subsequently, 307,111 nanopore reads 10,000–425,377 nt in length (total length 9,963,752,003) and representing a 62-fold genome coverage, were used in scaffolding step. The resulting MaSuRCA assembly consisted of 386 scaffolds (N50 408,333 bp, total length 191,862,084 bp). Scaffolds were assigned/ordered into pseudomolecules using the 7747 as reference for a Mauve Genome alignment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Darling, A. C., Mau, B., Blattner, F. R. &amp; Perna, N. T. Mauve: multiple alignment of conserved genomic sequence with rearrangements. Genome Res. 14, 1394–1403. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.2289704&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2805">33</a></sup>. Additionally, all the formation of super-scaffolds was accompanied by manual curation steps based on FISH results.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Gene prediction and functional annotation</h3><p>Gene finding was carried out using Gene Model Mapper (GeMoMa)—a similarity-based gene prediction program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Keilwagen, J. et al. Using intron position conservation for homology-based gene prediction. Nucl. Acids Res. 44, e89. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkw092&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2818">39</a></sup> (‘GeMoMa-1.6.1.jar CLI GeMoMaPipeline t = g = a = Extractor. p = true AnnotationFinalizer.r = SIMPLE AnnotationFinalizer.p = ’). Gene models were predicted by combining the predictions based on the genome data of the neighbor species <i>S. polyrhiza</i> and eight additional reference organisms (<i>S. polyrhiza</i> 7498 v3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Cao, H. X. et al. The map-based genome sequence of Spirodela polyrhiza aligned with its chromosomes, a reference for karyotype evolution. New Phytol. 209, 354–363. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/nph.13592&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2828">23</a></sup>, <i>Lemna</i> minor 5500<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Van Hoeck, A. et al. The first draft genome of the aquatic model plant Lemna minor opens the route for future stress physiology research and biotechnological applications. Biotech. Biofuels 8, 188. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1186/s13068-015-0381-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2836">38</a></sup>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> TAIR10, <i>Ananas comosus</i> (Phytozome internal code 321), <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (Phytozome internal code 314), <i>Nelumbo nucifera</i> 1.1 (GenBank assembly accession: GCF_000365185.1), <i>Panicum hallii</i> v3.1 (GenBank assembly accession: GCF_002211085.1), <i>Oryza sativa</i> IRGSP v1.0.38 (GenBank assembly accession: GCA_001433935.1), <i>Zostera marina</i> v2.1 (GenBank assembly accession: GCA_001185155.1)).</p><p>Noncoding RNAs were determined using RNAmmer v1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lagesen, K. et al. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucl. Acids Res. 35, 3100–3108. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkm160&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2865">55</a></sup> (‘rnammer -S euk -m tsu,ssu,lsu -h –f’) and tRNAscan-SE-2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Chan, P. P. &amp; Lowe, T. M. tRNAscan-SE: Searching for tRNA genes in genomic sequences. Methods Mol. Biol. 1–14, 2019. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/978-1-4939-9173-0_1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (1962)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2869">56</a></sup> (‘tRNAscan-SE -B -o -b –thread 10′). Predicted tRNAs were filtered for features overlapping with protein coding exons (‘bedtools intersect -b -a -wa -wb -f 0.8 -r’).</p><p>Functional annotations and GO terms were assigned using Interproscan 5 v 5.26–65.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btu031&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2876">57</a></sup> (‘interproscan.sh -dp -input -seqtype p -f tsv,html,gff3 -applications TIGRFAM, PfamA, SMART, SUPERFAMILY –pathways –goterms’). COGs (clusters of orthologous groups) were computed using eggnog-mapper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Huerta-Cepas, J. et al. Fast genome-wide functional annotation through orthology assignment by eggNOG-Mapper. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 2115–2122. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx148&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2880">58</a></sup> online tool (‘Taxonomic background: Viridiplantae, default options’) based on eggNOG 4.5 orthology data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Huerta-Cepas, J. et al. eggNOG 4.5: a hierarchical orthology framework with improved functional annotations for eukaryotic, prokaryotic and viral sequences. Nucl. Acids Res. 44, 286–293. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkv1248&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2884">59</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Comparative genomics and visualization</h3><p>Best bi-directional hits (BBHs) were identified using BLASTN 2.2.31 + (‘-evalue 1e-5 -out outfmt 6′). Additional filtering for length and percent identity was applied afterwards (minimum length: 100 nt; minimum identity: 80%). Annotation of gene features and repetitive elements in <i>S. polyrhiza</i> 9505 was taken from Michael et al., 2017<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Michael, T. P. et al. Comprehensive definition of genome features in Spirodela polyrhiza by high-depth physical mapping and short-read DNA sequencing strategies. Plant J. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13400&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2899">25</a></sup>. Both, length of selected repeat features and gene density were computed across a 0.5 Mbp window using the bedops suite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Neph, S. et al. BEDOPS: high-performance genomic feature operations. Bioinformatics 28, 1919–1920. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/bts277&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2903">60</a></sup>.</p><p>Feature annotation of each annotated repeats and genes as well as chromosome sizes have been converted to bed format using ‘gtf2bed’. From these files the 0.5 Mbp windows across each chromosome have been calculated with bedops –chop. Finally, the counts of gene and repeat features within each chromosome has been determined by bedmap –count.</p><p>Synteny was determined using minimap2 (‘minimap2 –x’)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 34, 3094–3100. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/bty191&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2913">35</a></sup> between <i>S. polyrhiza</i> and <i>S. intermedia</i>. 8410. Overlapping intervals have been merged by bedtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841–842. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btq033&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2923">61</a></sup> merge (default options). Links shorter than 10.000 have been excluded from the analysis.</p><p>To compare the two Spirodela genomes, synteny between pseudomolecules, gene and repeat distribution have been plotted using Circos tool v0.67-1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.092759.109&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2930">62</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Repeat annotation and analysis</h3><p>Repeat analysis in unassembled Illumina reads from the clone 8410 was performed using similarity-based clustering implemented in the RepeatExplorer pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Novak, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792–793. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btt054&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2942">40</a></sup>. The pipeline was run with modified settings in order to increase its sensitivity towards divergent repeats. The similarity search step was done with BLASTN instead of mgblast and the similarity threshold was lowered from 90 to 80% identity. A total of 2.4 million of 150 nt paired-end reads were used as an input, and repeats were annotated in clusters representing at least 0.005% of the input reads.</p><p>Repeat annotation in the assembled pseudomolecules was performed using several alternative approaches. PROFREP and DANTE modules available at the public RepeatExplorer server (<a href="https://repeatexplorer-elixir.cerit-sc.cz/">https://repeatexplorer-elixir.cerit-sc.cz/</a>) were used to annotate repeats based on similarities to the reference database compiled from the repeat clustering analysis described above, and to the REXdb database of conserved protein domains of mobile elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Neumann, P., Novak, P., Hostakova, N. &amp; Macas, J. Systematic survey of plant LTR-retrotransposons elucidates phylogenetic relationships of their polyprotein domains and provides a reference for element classification. Mob. DNA 10, 1. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1186/s13100-018-0144-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2956">63</a></sup>, respectively. Additionally, repetitive sequences in the assemblies were annotated using RepeatScout v. 1.0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; De Pevzner, P. A. novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21(Suppl 1), i351-358. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/bti1018&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2960">41</a></sup>. First, a library of repeats based on frequent k-mers was created using default parameters. Repeats with a frequency below 10 copies were then removed from the library. The resulting library was then used for annotating genome assemblies using RepeatMasker (<a href="https://www.repeatmasker.org">https://www.repeatmasker.org</a>).</p><p>Identification and quantification of microsatellites (simple sequence repeats) was done by means of Tandem Repeat Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucl. Acids Res. 27, 573–580. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/27.2.573&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2974">42</a></sup> using the settings “trf input_file 2 5 7 80 10 25 25 -f” and parsing the output using TRAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Sobreira, T. J., Durham, A. M. &amp; Gruber, A. TRAP: automated classification, quantification and annotation of tandemly repeated sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 361–362. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/bti809&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d46614e2978">64</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Molecular characterization of rDNA</h3><p>The estimation of 25S and 5S rRNA gene copies was carried out by qPCR, relating the rates of sample DNA amplification to the standard curve. The standard curve was constructed based on the amplification reads of dilution series of a reference plasmid, containing part of the <i>actin</i> gene (single copy in <i>Spirodela polyrhiza, 9509</i>), a whole PCR amplified 5S rDNA unit of <i>S. polyrhiza</i> and a part of the 25S gene amplified from genomic DNA of <i>S. polyrhiza</i>, 9509, using primers with internal restriction sites for XbaI and EcoRI. The rDNA copy number was determined in qPCR reactions prepared with the UltraSybr Mixture (CWBio, Taizhou, China), run on the CFX Connect Real-Time detection system (Bio-Rad, Hercules, USA). For quantification of the 25S rDNA we used the 5′-TCCCACTGTCCCTGTCTACT and 5′-CCCACTTATCCTACACCTCT primers, and for the 5S rDNA the set of primers was: 5′-GGGTGCGATCATACCAGCAC and 5′-GGGTGCAACACGAGGACTTC. The samples and tenfold dilution series of the reference plasmid were assayed in the same run. The quality of products was checked by thermal denaturation cycle. Only the experiments providing a single peak were considered. Three technical replicates were performed for each sample. The obtained data were analyzed using the program BIO-RAD CFX Manager 3.1 (Hercules, USA) and Microsoft Excel 2016 software.</p><p>For sequencing, the 5S rDNA genes were amplified from genomic DNA by PCR using 5S rRNA gene-specific primers DW-5S-F: CTTGGGCGAGAGTAGTACTAGG and DW-5S-R: CACGCTTAACTTCGGAGTTCTG, purified by gel electrophoresis and cloned into the vector pMD19 (Takara, Dalian, China). The obtained sequences were analyzed using the “Online Analysis Tools” package (<a href="https://molbiol-tools.ca">https://molbiol-tools.ca</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Mitotic chromosome preparation, probe preparation and FISH</h3><p>Spreading of mitotic chromosomes was carried out according to Hoang<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hoang, P. T. N. &amp; Schubert, I. Reconstruction of chromosome rearrangements between the two most ancestral duckweed species Spirodela polyrhiza and S. intermedia. Chromosoma 126, 729–739. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00412-017-0636-7&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3020">29</a></sup>. 5S rDNA, 18S and 26S rDNA probes were generated from <i>S. polyrhiza</i> and from <i>S. intermedia</i> genomic DNA each by using designed primer pairs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Tippery, N. P., Les, D. H. &amp; Crawford, D. J. Evaluation of phylogenetic relationships in Lemnaceae using nuclear ribosomal data. Plant Biol. 17(Suppl 1), 50–58. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/plb.12203&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3030">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Commun. 5, 3311. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms4311&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3033">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Kuzoff, R. K., Sweere, J. A., Soltis, D. E., Soltis, P. S. &amp; Zimmer, E. A. The phylogenetic potential of entire 26S rDNA sequences in plants. Mol. Biol. Evol. 15, 251–263 (1998)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3036">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Shoup, S. &amp; Lewis, L. A. Polyphyletic origin of parallel basal bodies in swimming cells of chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). J. Phycol. 39, 789–796. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1046/j.1529-8817.2003.03009.x&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3039">66</a></sup> as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hoang, P. N. T. et al. Generating a high-confidence reference genome map of the Greater Duckweed by integration of cytogenomic, optical mapping and Oxford Nanopore technologies. Plant J. 96, 670–684. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.14049&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3043">24</a></sup>. Ribosomal DNA, <i>A. thaliana</i> type telomere and <i>S. polyrhiza</i> BAC probes were labeled with Cy3-dUTP (GE Healthcare Life Science), Alexa Fluor 488-5-dUTP, Texas red-12-dUTP, biotin-dUTP or digoxigenin-dUTP (Life Technologies) and precipitated as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hoang, P. T. N. &amp; Schubert, I. Reconstruction of chromosome rearrangements between the two most ancestral duckweed species Spirodela polyrhiza and S. intermedia. Chromosoma 126, 729–739. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00412-017-0636-7&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3054">29</a></sup>.</p><p>Denaturation of mitotic chromosomes and probes, hybridization, post-hybridization washing and signal detection were carried out according to Lysak et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Lysak, M. A. et al. Mechanisms of chromosome number reduction in Arabidopsis thaliana and related Brassicaceae species. Proc. Nat. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 5224–5229. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.0510791103&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3061">67</a></sup>. Probe stripping and re-hybridization were done as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hoang, P. T. N. &amp; Schubert, I. Reconstruction of chromosome rearrangements between the two most ancestral duckweed species Spirodela polyrhiza and S. intermedia. Chromosoma 126, 729–739. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00412-017-0636-7&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3065">29</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Microscopy and image processing</h3><p>Fluorescence microscopy for signal detection followed Cao et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Cao, H. X. et al. The map-based genome sequence of Spirodela polyrhiza aligned with its chromosomes, a reference for karyotype evolution. New Phytol. 209, 354–363. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/nph.13592&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-75728-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d46614e3077">23</a></sup>. The images were processed (brightness and contrast adjustment only), pseudo-colored and merged using Adobe Photoshop software ver.12 × 32 (Adobe Systems).</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The genome assemblies of S. intermedia 7747 and 8410 have been deposited at the European Nucleotide Archive (ENA) under PRJEB35514 and PRJEB35634, respectively. Raw reads can be obtained from EBI ENA using accession numbers PRJEB33624 (PacBio, <i>S. intermedia</i> 7747), ERR3829756 (Illumina, <i>S. intermedia</i> 8410), and ERR3957957-ERR3957958 (Oxford Nanopore, <i>S. intermedia</i> 8410).</p>
